[2023-04-17 17:48:40] 856546017998929971: makes sense ya. the prompt is in english. the model is gpt style so all it knows is to continue audio. so if you give it spanish text, it's not obvious if the speaker speaks spanish or if a different speaker starts taking. you will probably see different variations as you run it multiple times. another thing you could try is to start with english text and then switch language. maybe even mid-sentence.
[2023-04-17 17:49:26] 856546017998929971: great to hear 🙂
[2023-04-17 17:51:45] 856546017998929971: the max duration is the full context, which is around ~14s for the public models. there is an EOS token which aborts sequence creation if it's produced by the model (this is to eg vocalize a short sentence) My guess is the model has usually seen only short prompts of music. It should be easy to prevent that if you use the generate functions directly and set `allow_early_stop=False`
[2023-04-17 17:52:44] 856546017998929971: yeah this is just a sliding window to stay somewhere in the middle of the generation and avoid edge effects. try `allow_early_stop=False` from my above answer, that should do the trick. you need the continued generation on the text-to-semantic side. the coarse model will just generate how ever long a sequence your semantic tokens are

[2023-04-19 22:35:37] 856546017998929971: something like this should work
```
from pydub import AudioSegment
int_audio_arr = (audio_arr * np.iinfo(np.int16).max).astype(np.int16)
audio_segment = AudioSegment(
    int_audio_arr.tobytes(),
    frame_rate=SAMPLE_RATE,
    sample_width=int_audio_arr.dtype.itemsize,
    channels=1,
)
audio_segment.export("my_test.mp3", format="mp3")
```

[2023-04-20 10:48:17] 614946962139250711: Even though the model can technically take it as input, because it was trained on 14 seconds, I think it loses the connection between the words and the audio if you stuff like 2 paragraphs in there.
[2023-04-20 10:49:02] 614946962139250711: But for best quality you probably want to just decide what is about 14 seconds, or less.

[2023-04-20 18:31:16] 614946962139250711: Continuing all 3 histories make a very smooth result, but it also seems to amplify bad trends, like static will slowly take over the clip. It's a bit less smooth but just using the same exact history for for all segments is surprisingly is hard to hear the difference, and it keeps resetting the context so long stuff is stable. Probably should only lock semantic history and let fine and coarse always update, will try later. I think the way to go is keep a full history of all segments histories, and then in the generation you can analyze the current segment and decide whether to revert back to a previous semantic history. One thing I'm planning on testing is branching long audio, so it generates multiple long clips with a tree of different histories.
[2023-04-20 18:34:04] 856546017998929971: yeah that makes total sense. this all gets better with the next larger models, but in the meantime we also had some success concatting the original speaker prompt together with a bit of history and feed that as the history. to mitigate the effects of drift but still retain coherence across multiple samples

[2023-04-21 07:45:25] 160900384615825410: ok I finally got it running, and I'll time the example script in the README
[2023-04-21 07:47:00] 160900384615825410: on a 3080 in WSL2:
```
from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio
from IPython.display import Audio
from scipy.io.wavfile import write as write_wav

text_prompt = """
     Hello, my name is Suno. And, uh — and I like pizza. [laughs] 
     But I also have other interests such as playing tic tac toe.
"""
audio_array = generate_audio(text_prompt)
Audio(audio_array, rate=SAMPLE_RATE)


write_wav("./test2.wav", SAMPLE_RATE, audio_array)
```

[2023-04-21 08:41:50] 614946962139250711: I used this, the idea you can set the wpm to the level of your content:
```
def estimate_spoken_time(text, wpm=150, time_limit=14):
    # Remove text within square brackets
    text_without_brackets = re.sub(r'\[.*?\]', '', text)
    
    words = text_without_brackets.split()
    word_count = len(words)
    time_in_seconds = (word_count / wpm) * 60
    
    if time_in_seconds > time_limit:
        return True, time_in_seconds
    else:
        return False, time_in_seconds
```

[2023-04-21 17:26:44] 1069853697221337138: (You can change where it says "en_speaker_1")
```
from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio
from IPython.display import Audio

text_prompt = """
    I have a silky smooth voice, and today I will tell you about 
    the exercise regimen of the common sloth.
"""
audio_array = generate_audio(text_prompt, history_prompt="en_speaker_1")
Audio(audio_array, rate=SAMPLE_RATE)

# If you also want a WAV file
from scipy.io.wavfile import write as write_wav

write_wav("/path/to/audio.wav", SAMPLE_RATE, audio_array)
```

# 🚀 BARK INFINITY 🎶

_(This is no longer just a command line wrapper...)_

**Now it's BARK INFINITY!** 🎉

## 🌟 Main Features 🌟

### 1. INFINITY VOICES 🔊🌈
Discover cool new voices, save them, and share them. Every audio clip saves a speaker.npz file with the voice. To reuse a voice, move the generated speaker.npz file (named the same as the .wav file) to the "prompts" directory inside "bark" where all the other .npz files are.

🔊 [With random celebrity appearances!](assets/filewebm)

### 2. INFINITY LENGTH 🎵🔄
Any length prompt and audio clips. Sometimes the final result is seamless, sometimes it's stable (but usually not both!).

🎵 [Now with Slowly Morphing Rick Rolls!](assets/filewebm)

### 🕺 Confused Travolta Mode 🕺
![Confused Travolta GIF](media/confused_travolta.gif)

Can your text-to-speech model stammer and stall like a student answering a question about a book they didn't read? Bark can. That's the human touch. The *semantic* touch.

## 💡 But Wait, There's More: Travolta Mode Isn't Just A Joke 💡

Are you tired of telling your TTS model what to say? Why not take a break and let your TTS model do the work for you. With enough patient and Confused Travolta Mode, Bark can complete your joke punchlines FOR you. Truly we live in the future. It might take 20 tries to get a hit, but the 19 failures are also *very* amusing so it's a win/win.

_**BARK INFINITY** is possible because Bark is such an amazingly simple and powerful model that even I could poke around easily._

[2023-04-21 20:28:45] 856546017998929971: just added a convenient way to re-use unconditionally generated clips as input history for another generation to keep the same voice (most of the time):
```
from bark import generate_audio, save_as_prompt

text_prompt = "A first sentence said by a random speaker."
full_generation, audio_arr = generate_audio(text_prompt, output_full=True)
save_as_prompt("test.npz", full_generation)

text_prompt = "A second sentence said by the same speaker."
audio_arr_2 = generate_audio(text_prompt, history_prompt="test.npz")
```
[2023-04-21 20:29:14] 856546017998929971: this way people can also share good generations as `.npz` files and others can re-use them as input prompts

[2023-04-22 15:49:51] 581075028515356672: - **Thai**: Thai is a Southeast Asian language with five tones which is similar to Chinese. Thai is mainly used in Thailand and also in Cambodia, Laos, and Myanmar to some extent.
- **Vietnamese**: Vietnamese also has six tones, with five of them having changed pitch and one being without tone. Vietnamese language is widely used in Vietnam and surrounding areas such as Cambodia and Laos.
- **Cantonese**: Cantonese is a Chinese dialect with a more complex tonal system in comparison to Standard Chinese, having seven tones in total. This language is mainly used in Southern China, including Guangdong, Guangxi, Hong Kong, and Macau.
- **Old Chinese**: Old Chinese refers to the ancient Chinese language from the 14th century BC to the 3rd century AD, which has nine different tones. Old Chinese is used in some Chinese historical documents, such as "The Book of Poetry" and "The Rites of Zhou".
- **Teochew**: Teochew is a dialect spoken in Fujian and Guangdong provinces in China. It has eight tones, with the last one being a low tone (sometimes referred to as a "descending tone"). Teochew is mainly used in Southeast coastal areas of China and in Southeast Asia.

[2023-04-22 23:49:00] 361196166370689025: this is what I am using if it helps anyone
[2023-04-22 23:49:38] 361196166370689025: ```python
# Import required libraries
import time
from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio  # Import Bark's text-to-audio model and sample rate
from IPython.display import Audio
from scipy.io.wavfile import write as write_wav
from playsound import playsound

# Define the text prompt to be converted into speech using Bark
text_prompt = """
    ♪ Havana, ooh na-na (ayy)
    Half of my heart is in Havana, ooh na-na (ayy, ayy)
    He took me back to East Atlanta, na-na-na, ah
    Oh, but my heart is in Havana (ayy)
    There's somethin' 'bout his manners (uh-huh)
    Havana, ooh-na-na (uh) ♪
    """

# Generate the audio array from the text prompt using the Bark generate_audio function
audio_array = generate_audio(text_prompt)

# Display the generated audio in Jupyter Notebook (if you're using it)
Audio(audio_array, rate=SAMPLE_RATE)

# Get the current timestamp and use it to create a unique filename
timestamp = int(time.time())
unique_filename = f"audio_{timestamp}.wav"

# Write the generated audio array to the unique WAV file
write_wav(unique_filename, SAMPLE_RATE, audio_array)

# Play the generated unique WAV file using playsound
playsound(unique_filename)
```

[2023-04-23 11:52:44] 856546017998929971: they work very similar to LLMs. during decoding top_k will restrict the output at every step to the most likely k results before sampling, and top_p will do the same based on cumulative probability. So you can think of them a bit similar as temperature for decoding. We haven't seen huge patterns with the parameters other than temperature, but if you find interesting stuff let us know!

[2023-04-24 14:15:29] 614946962139250711: Try this maybe (not tested but should be close)
IMPORTANT: Don't start the regular windows CMD or terminal. Start a new program that was installed called 'Miniforge Prompt' or 'Anaconda Prompt' or something like that.
```
conda update ffmpeg
conda create --name bark python=3.10
conda activate bark
(now the word base on the left should say bark instead)
conda install pytorch torchvision torchaudio pytorch-cuda=11.8 -c pytorch -c nvidia

git clone https://github.com/suno-ai/bark.git
cd bark

wget https://raw.githubusercontent.com/JonathanFly/bark/main/bark_speak.py

python bark_speak.py --text_prompt "Hello I am speaking."
```

[2023-04-24 17:02:55] 967422212153950318: how about getting it to check its work with something like this...... for chunk in text_chunks:
    # Keep trying until the speech recognition matches the original chunk
    while True:
        # Generate audio for the current chunk
        audio_array, _ = generate_audio(chunk)

        # Write the audio to a temporary file
        temp_file = "temp.wav"
        write_wav(temp_file, SAMPLE_RATE, audio_array)

        # Transcribe the audio using speech recognition
        with sr.AudioFile(temp_file) as source:
            audio = r.record(source)
        transcription = r.recognize_google(audio)

        # Check if the transcription matches the original chunk
        if transcription.lower() == chunk.lower():
            break

        # If the transcription doesn't match, wait and try again
        time.sleep(1)

    # Add the generated audio to the list
    audio_chunks.append(audio_array)

[2023-04-25 14:40:33] 553354167779852288: Conveniently I enjoy avoiding paying attention to my classes in which I happen to be in one right now... This may help.

Using these lines of code from Fictiverse's fork (https://github.com/Fictiverse/bark/blob/main/UI.py#L7-L10), I was able to make these changes to the generation.py code that allows for a more dynamic allowed prompts variable. 

```ALLOWED_PROMPTS = {"announcer"}

for _, lang in SUPPORTED_LANGS:
    for n in range(10):
        ALLOWED_PROMPTS.add(f"{lang}_speaker_{n}")

voicePath = "bark/assets/prompts"
files = os.listdir(voicePath)
npz_files = [f for f in files if f.endswith(".npz")]
npz_names = [os.path.splitext(f)[0] for f in npz_files]

for elements in npz_names:
    ALLOWED_PROMPTS.add(elements)```

The only addition that's really mine is just the for loop at the end haha. Hopefully this may help!

[2023-04-25 18:31:13] 201830562200027137: 1. Download miniconda and install
2. Download Cuda toolkit 11.8
3. conda create -n bark python=3.10
4. cd into the bark git pulled directory for this ui
5. conda activate bark
6. pip install .
6b. conda install -c conda-forge pysoundfile
7. conda install pytorch torchvision torchaudio pytorch-cuda=11.8 -c pytorch -c nvidia
8. conda install chardet
9. pip install cchardet
10. pip install gradio
11. python webui.py
12. Anytime you wanna fuck with it go into conda prompt cd into the directory "conda activate bark" > "python webui.py"
[2023-04-25 18:34:11] 553354167779852288: Ah! Then I highly recommend using this fork of bark: https://github.com/Fictiverse/bark/releases

It will install a virtual environment for you, which is basically like a small playground for your computer to use all the code it needs to run bark, without you having to do all the hard command line work.

Download the Bark_WebUI.7z file under assets at the link, and extract the file. 7z files can't be opened by Windows though, so you may need to go on Google and look up a program that can extract them. (I personally use the program 7zip.)

Then when you have extracted the code, run the run.bat file as an administrator, and it will do the hard work. It should only ask you once if you have an NVIDIA card. If you don't, or you feel like you don't have a strong enough card, then simply press "n" and enter when it asks. Otherwise, follow it's instructions.

[2023-04-26 13:58:46] 225795120878845954: use smaller models like this for example:

from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio, preload_models
from IPython.display import Audio

preload_models(
text_use_small=True,
coarse_use_small=True,
fine_use_small=True)

text_prompt = """
     Hello, my name is Suno. And, uh — and I like pizza. [laughs] 
     But I also have other interests such as playing tic tac toe.
"""
audio_array = generate_audio(text_prompt)

Audio(audio_array, rate=SAMPLE_RATE)

    Open the Start Menu and search for "Environment Variables"

    Click on the "Environment Variables" button
    Under "System Variables", scroll down and find the "Path" variable
    Click "Edit" and then "New"
    Enter the path to the folder containing the Conda executable (e.g. C:\ProgramData\Anaconda3\Scripts)
    Click "OK" to close all windows
    Restart your command prompt and try running the "conda" command again.

    _ = preload_models()
```
change to:
```
if not DEBUG_MODE:
    _ = preload_models(text_use_small=True,coarse_use_small=True,fine_use_small=True)
```

[2023-04-30 21:11:17] 1078496416416137377: I found this being discussed I wanted to know if you could use it to isolate voices somehow-

Devs need to post all the features instead of hiding them(voice cloning locked behind 3 lines or code)or just not mentioning them at all(using the same voice).
After you generate a voice, take its .npz file out of the bark_samples folder and put it in bark/assets/prompts. now run python bark_perform.py as usual but put --history_prompt nameofnpzfilehere at the end of it to reuse the same seed/settings file.
Congratulations! You can now use the same voice 

Keep in mind I didn't write that myself so I'm of no use, I just thought you might like it

[2023-04-30 22:55:23] 522374527783665664: It worked. THX. what I did:
```
from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio, preload_models
from IPython.display import Audio
import os # Added this line

os.environ['XDG_CACHE_HOME'] = '/content/drive/MyDrive/models/text-speech/suno_bark/models' # And this line
preload_models(fine_use_small=True, text_use_small=True,coarse_use_small=True)
```
